<a href="/wiki/Book:Thermodynamics" title="Book:Thermodynamics">Book:Thermodynamics</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Thermodynamics_sidebar" title="Template:Thermodynamics sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Thermodynamics_sidebar" title="Template talk:Thermodynamics sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Thermodynamics_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Heat capacity</b> or <b>thermal capacity</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">measurable</a> <a href="/wiki/Physical_quantity" title="Physical quantity">physical quantity</a> equal to the ratio of the <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a> added to (or removed from) an object to the resulting <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The unit of heat capacity is <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joule</a> per <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathrm {\tfrac {J}{K}} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mi mathvariant="normal">J</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">K</mi>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathrm {\tfrac {J}{K}} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathrm {\tfrac {J}{K}} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/12b417d4b4de6e79dd16ab09c36f75cdcba506b7" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:2.115ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span>, or <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilogram</a> <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">metre</a> squared per <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a> <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">second</a> squared <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathrm {\tfrac {kg\cdot m^{2}}{K\cdot s^{2}}} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">

<mn>2</mn>

<mrow>

<msup>
<mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathrm {\tfrac {kg\cdot m^{2}}{K\cdot s^{2}}} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathrm {\tfrac {kg\cdot m^{2}}{K\cdot s^{2}}} }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e11b3eb3d8a57adcb70fdfc6f2a1107a8cc2b1ea" style="vertical-align: -1.505ex; width:5.184ex; height:4.676ex;"/></span> in the <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">International System of Units</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI" title="SI">SI</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Dimensional_analysis" title="Dimensional analysis">dimensional form</a> is <span class="texhtml"><span style="font-family:sans-serif;font-style:normal;">L</span><sup>2</sup><span style="font-family:sans-serif;font-style:normal;">M</span><span style="font-family:sans-serif;font-style:normal;">T</span><sup>−2</sup><span style="font-family:sans-serif;font-style:normal;">Θ</span><sup>−1</sup></span>. Specific heat is the amount of heat needed to raise the temperature of one kilogram of <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> by 1 kelvin.
</p><p>Heat capacity is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extensive_property" title="Extensive property">extensive property</a> of matter, meaning that it is proportional to the size of the system. When expressing the same phenomenon as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intensive_property" title="Intensive property">intensive property</a>, the heat capacity is divided by the amount of substance, mass, or volume, thus the quantity is independent of the size or extent of the sample.  The <b>molar heat capacity</b> is the heat capacity per unit amount (SI unit: <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mole</a>) of a pure substance, and the <b>specific heat capacity</b>, often called simply <b>specific heat</b>, is the heat capacity per unit mass of a material. Nonetheless some authors use the term <b>specific heat</b> to refer to the ratio of the <b>specific heat capacity</b> of a substance at any given temperature to the <b>specific heat capacity</b> of another substance at a reference temperature, much in the fashion of <a href="/wiki/Specific_gravity" title="Specific gravity">specific gravity</a>. In some engineering contexts, the <a href="/wiki/Volumetric_heat_capacity" title="Volumetric heat capacity">volumetric heat capacity</a> is used.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">Temperature</a> reflects the average randomized kinetic <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> of constituent particles of matter (i.e., atoms or molecules) relative to the centre of mass of the system, while heat is the transfer of energy across a system boundary into the body other than by work or matter transfer. Translation, rotation, and vibration of atoms represent the <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(physics_and_chemistry)" title="Degrees of freedom (physics and chemistry)">degrees of freedom</a> of motion which classically contribute to the heat capacity of gases, while only vibrations are needed to describe the heat capacities of most solids,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> as shown by the <a href="/wiki/Dulong%E2%80%93Petit_law" title="Dulong–Petit law">Dulong–Petit law</a>. Other contributions can come from magnetic<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Electronic_entropy" title="Electronic entropy">electronic</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
degrees of freedom in solids, but these rarely make substantial contributions.
</p><p>For quantum mechanical reasons, at any given temperature, some of these degrees of freedom may be unavailable, or only partially available, to store thermal energy. In such cases, the heat capacity is a fraction of the maximum. As the temperature approaches <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">absolute zero</a>, the heat capacity of a system approaches zero because of loss of available degrees of freedom. <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">Quantum theory</a> can be used to quantitatively predict the heat capacity of simple systems.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_heat" title="History of heat">History of heat</a></div>
<p>In a previous theory of heat common in the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a>, heat was thought to be a measurement of an invisible fluid, known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Caloric_theory" title="Caloric theory">caloric</a></i>.  Bodies were capable of holding a certain amount of this fluid, hence the term <i>heat capacity</i>, named and first investigated by <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scottish</a> chemist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Black" title="Joseph Black">Joseph Black</a> in the 1750s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laider_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laider-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the development of <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamics" title="Thermodynamics">thermodynamics</a> in the 18th and 19th centuries, scientists have abandoned the idea of a physical caloric, and instead understand <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a> as a manifestation of a system's <a href="/wiki/Internal_energy" title="Internal energy">internal energy</a>. Heat is no longer considered a fluid, but rather a transfer of disordered energy. Nevertheless, at least in English, the term "heat capacity" survives. In some other languages, the term <i>thermal capacity</i> is preferred, and it is also sometimes used in English.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Units">Units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extensive_properties">Extensive properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Extensive properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the International System of Units, heat capacity has the unit joules per Kelvin (J/K). The heat capacity (symbol <i>C</i>) of a system is defined as the ratio of heat transferred to or from the system and the resulting change in temperature in the system,
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C(T)={\frac {\delta Q}{dT}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>C</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>T</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi>δ<!-- δ --></mi>
<mi>Q</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mi>d</mi>
<mi>T</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C(T)={\frac {\delta Q}{dT}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle C(T)={\frac {\delta Q}{dT}},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d0c0fadbf46e46c8a8dc91d11bf20415df4cb372" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:12.68ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where the symbol <i>δ</i> designates heat as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Path_function" title="Path function">path function</a>. 
If the temperature change is sufficiently small the heat capacity may be assumed to be constant:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C={\frac {Q}{\Delta T}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C={\frac {Q}{\Delta T}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle C={\frac {Q}{\Delta T}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/13b945c66b13fbfd4e8d60644f7ed5e79e135331" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:9.92ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>Heat capacity is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extensive_property" title="Extensive property">extensive property</a>, meaning it depends on the extent or size of the physical system studied. A sample containing twice the amount of substance as another sample requires the transfer of twice the amount of heat (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle Q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>Q</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Q}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="Q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8752c7023b4b3286800fe3238271bbca681219ed" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.838ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>) to achieve the same change in temperature (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Delta T}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi mathvariant="normal">Δ<!-- Δ --></mi>
<mi>T</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \Delta T}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\Delta T" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e61e7deb9c7c7b7dda762b0935e757add2acc559" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.572ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intensive_properties">Intensive properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Intensive properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For many purposes it is more convenient to report heat capacity as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intensive_property" title="Intensive property">intensive property</a>, an intrinsic characteristic of a particular substance. In practice, this is most often an expression of the property in relation to a unit of <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>; in science and engineering, such properties are often prefixed with the term <i>specific</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> International standards now recommend that specific heat capacity always refer to division by mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The units for the <b>specific heat capacity</b> are <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle [c]=\mathrm {\tfrac {J}{kg\cdot K}} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo stretchy="false">[</mo>
<mi>c</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">]</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mi mathvariant="normal">J</mi>
<mrow>
<mi mathvariant="normal">k</mi>
<mi mathvariant="normal">g</mi>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">K</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle [c]=\mathrm {\tfrac {J}{kg\cdot K}} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="[c]=\mathrm {\tfrac {J}{kg\cdot K}} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/59f89f7dd4fd480785b25d2b47db12d21704d6ec" style="vertical-align: -1.671ex; width:9.661ex; height:4.176ex;"/></span>.
</p><p>In chemistry, heat capacity is often specified relative to one mole, the unit of <a href="/wiki/Amount_of_substance" title="Amount of substance">amount of substance</a>, and is called the <b>molar heat capacity</b>. It has the unit <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle [C_{\mathrm {mol} }]=\mathrm {\tfrac {J}{mol\cdot K}} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle [C_{\mathrm {mol} }]=\mathrm {\tfrac {J}{mol\cdot K}} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="[C_{\mathrm {mol} }]=\mathrm {\tfrac {J}{mol\cdot K}} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c6ce0541306c6ba08bf56f477816d781fbce85bb" style="vertical-align: -1.338ex; width:14.155ex; height:3.843ex;"/></span>.
</p><p>For some considerations it is useful to specify the volume-specific heat capacity, commonly called <a href="/wiki/Volumetric_heat_capacity" title="Volumetric heat capacity">volumetric heat capacity</a>, which is the heat capacity per unit volume and has SI units <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle [s]=\mathrm {\tfrac {J}{m^{3}\cdot K}} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

This is used almost exclusively for liquids and solids, since for gases it may be confused with specific heat capacity <i>at constant volume</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_unit_systems">Alternative unit systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Alternative unit systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While SI units are the most widely used, some countries and industries also use other systems of measurement. One older unit of heat is the <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">kilogram-calorie</a> (Cal), originally defined as the energy required to raise the temperature of one kilogram of water by one degree Celsius, typically from 14.5 to 15.5 °C.  The specific average heat capacity of water on this scale would therefore be exactly 1 Cal/(C°⋅kg).  However, due to the temperature-dependence of the specific heat, a large number of different definitions of the calorie came into being. Whilst once it was very prevalent, especially its smaller <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cgs" title="Cgs">cgs</a> variant the gram-calorie (cal), defined thus the specific heat of water would be 1 cal/(K⋅g), in most fields the use of the calorie is now archaic.
</p><p>In the United States other units of measure for heat capacity may be quoted in disciplines such as <a href="/wiki/Construction" title="Construction">construction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Civil_engineering" title="Civil engineering">civil engineering</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chemical_engineering" title="Chemical engineering">chemical engineering</a>. A still common system is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Engineering_Units" title="English Engineering Units">English Engineering Units</a> in which the mass reference is <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">pound mass</a> and the temperature is specified in <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">degrees Fahrenheit</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rankine_scale" title="Rankine scale">Rankine</a>. One (rare) unit of heat is the pound calorie (lb-cal), defined as the amount of heat required to raise the temperature of one pound of water by one degree Celsius. On this scale the specific heat of water would be 1 lb-cal/(K⋅lb).  More common is the <a href="/wiki/British_thermal_unit" title="British thermal unit">British thermal unit</a>, the standard unit of heat in the U.S. construction industry. This is defined such that the specific heat of water is 1 BTU/(F°⋅lb).
The path integral Monte Carlo method is a numerical approach for determining the values of heat capacity, based on quantum dynamical principles. However, good approximations can be made for gases in many states using simpler methods outlined below. For many solids composed of relatively heavy atoms (atomic number &gt; iron), at non-cryogenic temperatures, the heat capacity at room temperature approaches 3R = 24.94 joules per kelvin per mole of atoms (Dulong–Petit law, R is the gas constant). Low temperature approximations for both gases and solids at temperatures less than their characteristic Einstein temperatures or Debye temperatures can be made by the methods of Einstein and Debye discussed below.
Water (liquid): CP = 4185.5 J/(kg⋅K) (15 °C, 101.325 kPa)
Water (liquid): CVH = 74.539 J/(mol⋅K) (25 °C)
For liquids and gases, it is important to know the pressure to which given heat capacity data refer. Most published data are given for standard pressure. However,  different standard conditions for temperature and pressure have been defined by different organizations. The International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry (IUPAC) changed its recommendation from one atmosphere to the round value 100 kPa (≈750.062 Torr).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gold_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-gold-8">[notes 1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement">Measurement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Measurement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>It may appear that the way to measure heat capacity is to add a known amount of heat to an object, and measure the change in temperature. This works reasonably well for many solids. However, for precise measurements, and especially for gases, other aspects of measurement become critical.
</p><p>The heat capacity can be affected by many of the <a href="/wiki/State_variable" title="State variable">state variables</a> that describe the thermodynamic system under study. These include the starting and ending temperature, as well as the pressure and the volume of the system before and after heat is added. So rather than a single way to measure heat capacity, there are actually several slightly different measurements of heat capacity. The most commonly used methods for measurement are to hold the object either at constant <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a>
(<i>C</i><sub>P</sub>) or at constant <a href="/wiki/Volume_(thermodynamics)" title="Volume (thermodynamics)">volume</a> (<i>C</i><sub>V</sub>). <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">Gases</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquids</a> are typically also measured at constant volume. Measurements under constant pressure produce larger values than those at constant volume because the constant pressure values also include heat energy that is used to do <a href="/wiki/Work_(thermodynamics)" title="Work (thermodynamics)">work</a> to expand the substance against the constant pressure as its temperature increases. This difference is particularly notable in gases where values under constant pressure are typically 30% to 66.7% greater than those at constant volume. Hence the <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity_ratio" title="Heat capacity ratio">heat capacity ratio</a> of gases is typically between 1.3 and 1.67.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lange_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lange-9">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The specific heat capacities of substances comprising molecules (as distinct from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monatomic" title="Monatomic">monatomic</a> gases) are not fixed constants and vary somewhat depending on temperature. Accordingly, the temperature at which the measurement is made is usually also specified. Examples of two common ways to cite the specific heat of a substance are as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toolbox_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-toolbox-10">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Water (liquid): <i>C</i><sub>P</sub> = 4185.5 J/(kg⋅K) (15 °C, 101.325 kPa)</li>
<li>Water (liquid): <i>C</i><sub>V</sub><i>H</i> = 74.539 J/(mol⋅K) (25 °C)</li></ul>
<p>For liquids and gases, it is important to know the pressure to which given heat capacity data refer. Most published data are given for standard pressure. However, quite different <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">standard conditions for temperature and pressure</a> have been defined by different organizations. The <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry</a> (IUPAC) changed its recommendation from one <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">atmosphere</a> to the round value 100 kPa (≈750.062 Torr).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gold_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-gold-8">[notes 1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calculation_from_first_principles">Calculation from first principles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Calculation from first principles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Path_integral_Monte_Carlo" title="Path integral Monte Carlo">path integral Monte Carlo</a> method is a numerical approach for determining the values of heat capacity, based on quantum dynamical principles. However, good approximations can be made for gases in many states using simpler methods outlined below. For many solids composed of relatively heavy atoms (atomic number &gt; iron), at non-cryogenic temperatures, the heat capacity at room temperature approaches 3<i>R</i> = 24.94 joules per kelvin per mole of atoms (<a href="/wiki/Dulong%E2%80%93Petit_law" title="Dulong–Petit law">Dulong–Petit law</a>, <i>R</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Gas_constant" title="Gas constant">gas constant</a>). Low temperature approximations for both gases and solids at temperatures less than their characteristic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einstein_temperature" title="Einstein temperature">Einstein temperatures</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Debye_temperature" title="Debye temperature">Debye temperatures</a> can be made by the methods of Einstein and Debye discussed below.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermodynamic_relations_and_definition_of_heat_capacity">Thermodynamic relations and definition of heat capacity <span id="Heat_capacity"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Thermodynamic relations and definition of heat capacity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>are property relations and are therefore independent of the type of process. In other words, they are valid for any substance going through any process. Both the internal energy and enthalpy of a substance can change with the transfer of energy in many forms i.e., heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_between_heat_capacities">Relation between heat capacities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Relation between heat capacities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Relations_between_heat_capacities" title="Relations between heat capacities">Relations between heat capacities</a></div>
<p>Measuring the heat capacity, sometimes referred to as specific heat, at constant volume can be prohibitively difficult for liquids and solids. That is, small temperature changes typically require large pressures to maintain a liquid or solid at constant volume, implying that the containing vessel must be nearly rigid or at least very strong (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coefficient_of_thermal_expansion" title="Coefficient of thermal expansion">coefficient of thermal expansion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Compressibility" title="Compressibility">compressibility</a>). Instead, it is easier to measure the heat capacity at constant pressure (allowing the material to expand or contract freely) and solve for the heat capacity at constant volume using mathematical relationships derived from the basic thermodynamic laws. Starting from the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_thermodynamic_relation" title="Fundamental thermodynamic relation">fundamental thermodynamic relation</a> one can show that
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C_{P}-C_{V}=T\left({\frac {\partial P}{\partial T}}\right)_{V,n}\left({\frac {\partial V}{\partial T}}\right)_{P,n},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>P</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>V</mi>

<mo>=</mo>
<mi>T</mi>

<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>

</mrow>

</mrow>
<mo>)</mo>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C_{P}-C_{V}=T\left({\frac {\partial P}{\partial T}}\right)_{V,n}\left({\frac {\partial V}{\partial T}}\right)_{P,n},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle C_{P}-C_{V}=T\left({\frac {\partial P}{\partial T}}\right)_{V,n}\left({\frac {\partial V}{\partial T}}\right)_{P,n},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7c08d184ec9cd557cc84ea2606eefc7407c17696" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:35.041ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where the partial derivatives are taken at constant volume and constant number of particles, and constant pressure and constant number of particles, respectively.
</p><p>This can also be rewritten as
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C_{P}-C_{V}=VT{\frac {\alpha ^{2}}{\beta _{T}}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</msup>

<p>where
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \alpha }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mi>α<!-- α --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \alpha }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\alpha " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b79333175c8b3f0840bfb4ec41b8072c83ea88d3" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.488ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coefficient_of_thermal_expansion" title="Coefficient of thermal expansion">coefficient of thermal expansion</a>,</dd>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \beta _{T}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>β<!-- β --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>T</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \beta _{T}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\beta _{T}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3f2855fc59047bc00f5bdedbcad68550a7ac6d13" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.705ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isothermal" title="Isothermal">isothermal</a> <a href="/wiki/Compressibility" title="Compressibility">compressibility</a>.</dd></dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity_ratio" title="Heat capacity ratio">heat capacity ratio</a>, or adiabatic index, is the ratio of the heat capacity at constant pressure to heat capacity at constant volume. It is sometimes also known as the isentropic expansion factor.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideal_gas">Ideal gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ideal gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Specific_heat_capacity">Specific heat capacity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Specific heat capacity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The specific heat capacity of a material on a per mass basis is
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle c={\frac {\partial C}{\partial m}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∂<!-- ∂ --></mi>
<mi>C</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∂<!-- ∂ --></mi>
<mi>m</mi>

<p>where
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle m}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>For gases, and also for other materials under high pressures, there is need to distinguish between different boundary conditions for the processes under consideration (since values differ significantly between different conditions). Typical processes for which a heat capacity may be defined include <a href="/wiki/Isobaric_process" title="Isobaric process">isobaric</a> (constant pressure, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\text{d}}P=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>d</mtext>
</mrow>
<mi>P</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\text{d}}P=0}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\text{d}P = 0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2997415b7c242bcf6f4ebf7ef17957d9df03a9aa" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:7.299ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>) or <a href="/wiki/Isochoric_process" title="Isochoric process">isochoric</a> (constant volume, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\text{d}}V=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>d</mtext>
</mrow>
<mi>V</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\text{d}}V=0}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\text{d}V = 0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3e19ded03242b58e141dced9f6f46c3a8829e436" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:7.341ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>) processes. The corresponding specific heat capacities are expressed as
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle c_{P}=\left({\frac {\partial C}{\partial m}}\right)_{P},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∂<!-- ∂ --></mi>
<mi>C</mi>
</mrow>

<mi>m</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>

<mi>P</mi>

<semantics>

<p>A related parameter to <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle c}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

In engineering practice, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle c_{V}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

In such cases, the mass-specific heat capacity (specific heat) is often explicitly written with the subscript <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle m}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>For pure <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homogeneous" title="Homogeneous">homogeneous</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compounds</a> with established <a href="/wiki/Molecular_mass" title="Molecular mass">molecular or molar mass</a>, or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mole_(chemistry)" title="Mole (chemistry)">molar quantity</a>, heat capacity as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intensive_property" title="Intensive property">intensive property</a> can be expressed on a per-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mole_(chemistry)" title="Mole (chemistry)">mole</a> basis instead of a per-mass basis by the following equations analogous to the per mass equations:

<p>where <i>n</i> is the number of moles in the body or <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_system" title="Thermodynamic system">thermodynamic system</a>. One may refer to such a per-mole quantity as <b>molar heat capacity</b> to distinguish it from specific heat capacity on a per-mass basis.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polytropic_heat_capacity">Polytropic heat capacity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Polytropic heat capacity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polytropic" title="Polytropic">polytropic</a> heat capacity is calculated at processes if all the thermodynamic properties (pressure, volume, temperature) change:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C_{i,m}=\left({\frac {\partial C}{\partial n}}\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>m</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∂<!-- ∂ --></mi>
<mi>C</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∂<!-- ∂ --></mi>
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>)</mo>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C_{i,m}=\left({\frac {\partial C}{\partial n}}\right)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="C_{i,m} = \left(\frac{\partial C}{\partial n}\right)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f193e658027043ddd9b1041a97fc221ff61c860a" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:14.802ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span> = molar heat capacity at polytropic process.</dd></dl>
<p>The most important polytropic processes run between the adiabatic and the isotherm functions, the polytropic index is between 1 and the adiabatic exponent (γ or κ).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dimensionless_heat_capacity">Dimensionless heat capacity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Dimensionless heat capacity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dimensionless_number" title="Dimensionless number">dimensionless</a> heat capacity of a material is
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C^{*}={\frac {C}{nR}}={\frac {C}{Nk}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>∗<!-- ∗ --></mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow>
<mi>n</mi>
<mi>R</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow>
<mi>N</mi>
<mi>k</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C^{*}={\frac {C}{nR}}={\frac {C}{Nk}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle C^{*}={\frac {C}{nR}}={\frac {C}{Nk}},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c2477ae71068330cf61e7ca1242aaba44c179db1" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:17.802ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where
</p>
<dl><dd><i>C</i> is the heat capacity of a body made of the material in question (J/K),</dd>
<dd><i>n</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Amount_of_substance" title="Amount of substance">amount of substance</a> in the body (<a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mol</a>),</dd>
<dd><i>R</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Gas_constant" title="Gas constant">gas constant</a> (J/(K⋅mol)),</dd>
<dd><i>N</i> is the number of molecules in the body (dimensionless),</dd>
<dd><i>k</i> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boltzmann%27s_constant" title="Boltzmann's constant">Boltzmann's constant</a> (J/(K⋅molecule)).</dd></dl>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">ideal gas</a> article, dimensionless heat capacity <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C^{*}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>∗<!-- ∗ --></mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C^{*}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="C^*" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9fda87fa9eddc6a89e202bdebaa9a5e1a55dec9d" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.852ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> is expressed as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\hat {c}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

This holds true for quadratic degrees of freedom, a consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Equipartition_theorem" title="Equipartition theorem">equipartition theorem</a>.
</p><p>More generally, the dimensionless heat capacity relates the logarithmic increase in temperature to the increase in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dimensionless_entropy" title="Dimensionless entropy">dimensionless entropy</a> per particle <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle S^{*}=S/Nk}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C^{*}={\frac {{\text{d}}S^{*}}{{\text{d}}(\ln T)}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>Alternatively, using base-2 logarithms, <i>C</i><sup>*</sup> relates the base-2 logarithmic increase in temperature to the increase in the dimensionless entropy measured in <a href="/wiki/Bit" title="Bit">bits</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heat_capacity_at_absolute_zero">Heat capacity at absolute zero</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Heat capacity at absolute zero">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From the definition of <a href="/wiki/Entropy#Thermodynamic_definition_of_entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle T\,{\text{d}}S=\delta Q,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>T</mi>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>d</mtext>
</mrow>
<mi>S</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>δ<!-- δ --></mi>
<mi>Q</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle T\,{\text{d}}S=\delta Q,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle T\,{\text{d}}S=\delta Q,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5cca7738465956fa4c1d790333af389a15b667c7" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:11.447ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>the absolute entropy can be calculated by integrating from zero to the final temperature <i>T</i><sub>f</sub>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle S(T_{\text{f}})=\int _{T=0}^{T_{\text{f}}}{\frac {\delta Q}{T}}=\int _{0}^{T_{\text{f}}}{\frac {\delta Q}{{\text{d}}T}}{\frac {{\text{d}}T}{T}}=\int _{0}^{T_{\text{f}}}C(T)\,{\frac {{\text{d}}T}{T}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>S</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<msub>
<mi>T</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>f</mtext>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<msubsup>
<mo>∫<!-- ∫ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>T</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msub>
<mi>T</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>f</mtext>
</mrow>
