<p>The heat capacity must be zero at zero temperature in order for the above integral not to yield an infinite absolute entropy, which would violate the <a href="/wiki/Third_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Third law of thermodynamics">third law of thermodynamics</a>. One of the strengths of the <a href="/wiki/Debye_model" title="Debye model">Debye model</a> is that (unlike the preceding Einstein model) it predicts the proper mathematical form of the approach of heat capacity toward zero, as absolute zero temperature is approached.
</p>
<h3><span id="Negative_heat_capacity_.28stars.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Negative_heat_capacity_(stars)">Negative heat capacity (stars)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Negative heat capacity (stars)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most physical systems exhibit a positive heat capacity.  However, even though it can seem paradoxical at first,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> there are some systems for which the heat capacity is <i>negative</i>.  These are inhomogeneous systems that do not meet the strict definition of thermodynamic equilibrium. They include gravitating objects such as stars and galaxies, and also sometimes some <a href="/wiki/Nanoscopic_scale" title="Nanoscopic scale">nano-scale</a> clusters of a few tens of atoms, close to a phase transition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup>  A negative heat capacity can result in a <a href="/wiki/Negative_temperature" title="Negative temperature">negative temperature</a>.
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Virial_theorem" title="Virial theorem">virial theorem</a>, for a self-gravitating body like a star or an interstellar gas cloud, the average potential energy <i>U</i><sub>pot</sub> and the average kinetic energy <i>U</i><sub>kin</sub> are locked together in the relation
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle U_{\text{pot}}=-2U_{\text{kin}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>U</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>pot</mtext>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<msub>
<mi>U</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>kin</mtext>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle U_{\text{pot}}=-2U_{\text{kin}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle U_{\text{pot}}=-2U_{\text{kin}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e113908dcfa06559897a3f6876690ef9cde937c0" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:14.97ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>The total energy <i>U</i> (= <i>U</i><sub>pot</sub> + <i>U</i><sub>kin</sub>) therefore obeys
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle U=-U_{\text{kin}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>U</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>U</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>kin</mtext>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle U=-U_{\text{kin}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle U=-U_{\text{kin}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9c5688976d62e680b87c03c10a8566629db9e6f0" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:11.395ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>If the system loses energy, for example, by radiating energy away into space, the average kinetic energy actually increases.  If a temperature is defined by the average kinetic energy, then the system therefore can be said to have a negative heat capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>A more extreme version of this occurs with <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black holes</a>.  According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black-hole_thermodynamics" title="Black-hole thermodynamics">black-hole thermodynamics</a>, the more mass and energy a black hole absorbs, the colder it becomes. In contrast, if it is a net emitter of energy, through <a href="/wiki/Hawking_radiation" title="Hawking radiation">Hawking radiation</a>, it will become hotter and hotter until it boils away.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theory">Theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Factors_that_affect_specific_heat_capacity">Factors that affect specific heat capacity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Factors that affect specific heat capacity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:268px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thermally_Agitated_Molecule.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="280" data-file-width="280" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Thermally_Agitated_Molecule.gif/266px-Thermally_Agitated_Molecule.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/23/Thermally_Agitated_Molecule.gif 1.5x" width="266"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thermally_Agitated_Molecule.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Molecules undergo many characteristic internal vibrations. Potential energy stored in these internal degrees of freedom contributes to a sample’s energy content, <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> but not to its temperature. More internal degrees of freedom tend to increase a substance's specific heat capacity, so long as temperatures are high enough to overcome quantum effects.</div></div></div>
<p>For any given substance, the heat capacity of a body is directly proportional to the amount of substance it contains (measured in terms of mass or moles or volume). Doubling the amount of substance in a body doubles its heat capacity, etc.
</p><p>However, when this effect has been corrected for, by dividing the heat capacity by the quantity of substance in a body, the resulting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" title="Specific heat capacity">specific heat capacity</a> is a function of the structure of the substance itself. In particular, it depends on the number of <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(physics_and_chemistry)" title="Degrees of freedom (physics and chemistry)">degrees of freedom</a> that are available to the particles in the substance; each independent degree of freedom allows the particles to store thermal energy. The translational <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> of substance particles which manifests as <i>temperature change</i> is only one of the many possible degrees of freedom, and thus the larger the number of degrees of freedom available to the particles of a substance <i>other</i> than translational kinetic energy, the larger will be the specific heat capacity for the substance. For example, rotational kinetic energy of gas molecules stores heat energy in a way that increases heat capacity, since this energy does not contribute to temperature.
</p><p>In addition, quantum effects require that whenever energy be stored in any mechanism associated with a bound system which confers a degree of freedom, it must be stored in certain minimal-sized deposits (quanta) of energy, or else not stored at all. Such effects limit the full ability of some degrees of freedom to store energy when their lowest energy storage quantum amount is not easily supplied at the average energy of particles at a given temperature. In general, for this reason, specific heat capacities tend to fall at lower temperatures where the average thermal energy available to each particle degree of freedom is smaller, and thermal energy storage begins to be limited by these quantum effects. Due to this process, as temperature falls toward absolute zero, so also does heat capacity.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Degrees_of_freedom">Degrees of freedom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Degrees of freedom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(February 2015)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(physics_and_chemistry)" title="Degrees of freedom (physics and chemistry)">degrees of freedom (physics and chemistry)</a></div>
<p>Molecules are quite different from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monatomic" title="Monatomic">monatomic</a> gases like <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>. With monatomic gases, thermal energy comprises only translational motions. Translational motions are ordinary, whole-body movements in <a href="/wiki/Three-dimensional_space" title="Three-dimensional space">3D space</a> whereby particles move about and exchange energy in collisions—like rubber balls in a vigorously shaken container (see animation here <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup>). These simple movements in the three dimensions of space mean individual atoms have three translational <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(physics_and_chemistry)" title="Degrees of freedom (physics and chemistry)">degrees of freedom</a>. A degree of freedom is any form of energy in which heat transferred into an object can be stored. This can be in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Translational_kinetic_energy" title="Translational kinetic energy">translational kinetic energy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotational_kinetic_energy" title="Rotational kinetic energy">rotational kinetic energy</a>, or other forms such as <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential energy</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vibrational_modes" title="Vibrational modes">vibrational modes</a>. Only three translational degrees of freedom (corresponding to the three independent directions in space) are available for any individual atom, whether it is free, as a monatomic molecule, or bound into a polyatomic molecule.
</p><p>As to rotation about an atom's axis (again, whether the atom is bound or free), its energy of rotation is proportional to the <a href="/wiki/Moment_of_inertia" title="Moment of inertia">moment of inertia</a> for the atom, which is extremely small compared to moments of inertia of collections of atoms. This is because almost all of the mass of a single atom is concentrated in its nucleus, which has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_radius" title="Nuclear radius">radius</a> too small to give a significant moment of inertia. In contrast, the <i>spacing</i> of quantum energy levels for a rotating object is inversely proportional to its moment of inertia, and so this spacing becomes very large for objects with very small moments of inertia. For these reasons, the contribution from rotation of atoms on their axes is essentially zero in monatomic gases, because the energy spacing of the associated quantum levels is too large for significant thermal energy to be stored in rotation of systems with such small moments of inertia. For similar reasons, axial rotation around bonds joining atoms in diatomic gases (or along the linear axis in a linear molecule of any length) can also be neglected as a possible "degree of freedom" as well, since such rotation is similar to rotation of monatomic atoms, and so occurs about an axis with a moment of inertia too small to be able to store significant heat energy.
</p><p>In polyatomic molecules, other rotational modes may become active, due to the much higher moments of inertia about certain axes which do not coincide with the linear axis of a linear molecule. These modes take the place of some translational degrees of freedom for individual atoms, since the atoms are moving in 3-D space, as the molecule rotates. The narrowing of quantum mechanically determined energy spacing between rotational states results from situations where atoms are rotating around an axis that does not connect them, and thus form an assembly that has a large moment of inertia. This small difference between energy states allows the kinetic energy of this type of rotational motion to store heat energy at ambient temperatures. Furthermore, internal vibrational degrees of freedom also may become active (these are also a type of translation, as seen from the view of each atom). In summary, molecules are complex objects with a population of atoms that may move about within the molecule in a number of different ways (see animation at right), and each of these ways of moving is capable of storing energy if the temperature is sufficient.
</p><p>The heat capacity of molecular substances (on a "per-atom" or atom-molar, basis) does not exceed the heat capacity of monatomic gases, unless vibrational modes are brought into play. The reason for this is that vibrational modes allow energy to be stored as potential energy in inter-atomic bonds in a molecule, which are not available to atoms in monatomic gases. Up to about twice as much energy (on a per-atom basis) per unit of temperature increase can be stored in a solid as in a monatomic gas, by this mechanism of storing energy in the potentials of interatomic bonds. This gives many solids about twice the atom-molar heat capacity at room temperature of monatomic gases.
</p><p>However, quantum effects heavily affect the actual ratio at lower temperatures (i.e., much lower than the melting temperature of the solid), especially in solids with light and tightly bound atoms (e.g., beryllium metal or diamond). Polyatomic gases store intermediate amounts of energy, giving them a "per-atom" heat capacity that is between that of monatomic gases (​<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>3</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> <i>R</i> per mole of atoms, where <i>R</i> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ideal_gas_constant" title="Ideal gas constant">ideal gas constant</a>), and the maximum of fully excited warmer solids (3 <i>R</i> per mole of atoms). For gases, heat capacity never falls below the minimum of ​<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>3</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> <i>R</i> per mole (of molecules), since the kinetic energy of gas molecules is always available to store at least this much thermal energy. However, at cryogenic temperatures in solids, heat capacity falls toward zero, as temperature approaches absolute zero.
</p>
<h4><span id="Example_of_temperature-dependent_specific_heat_capacity.2C_in_a_diatomic_gas"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Example_of_temperature-dependent_specific_heat_capacity,_in_a_diatomic_gas">Example of temperature-dependent specific heat capacity, in a diatomic gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Example of temperature-dependent specific heat capacity, in a diatomic gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>To illustrate the role of various degrees of freedom in storing heat, we may consider <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diatomic" title="Diatomic">diatomic</a> molecule that has five active degrees of freedom at room temperature: the three comprising translational motions plus two rotational degrees of freedom internally. Although the constant-volume molar heat capacity of nitrogen at this temperature is five-thirds that of monatomic gases, on a per-mole of atoms basis, it is five-sixths that of a monatomic gas. The reason for this is the loss of a degree of freedom due to the bond when it does not allow storage of thermal energy. Two separate nitrogen atoms would have a total of six degrees of freedom—the three translational degrees of freedom of each atom. When the atoms are bonded the molecule will still only have three translational degrees of freedom, as the two atoms in the molecule move as one. However, the molecule cannot be treated as a point object, and the moment of inertia has increased sufficiently about two axes to allow two rotational degrees of freedom to be active at room temperature to give five degrees of freedom. The moment of inertia about the third axis remains small, as this is the axis passing through the centres of the two atoms, and so is similar to the small moment of inertia for atoms of a monatomic gas. Thus, this degree of freedom does not act to store heat, and does not contribute to the heat capacity of nitrogen. The heat capacity <i>per atom</i> for nitrogen (5/2 R per mole molecules = 5/4 R per mole atoms) is therefore less than for a monatomic gas (3/2 R per mole molecules or atoms), so long as the temperature remains low enough that no vibrational degrees of freedom are activated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>At higher temperatures, however, nitrogen gas gains one more degree of internal freedom, as the molecule is excited into higher vibrational modes that store thermal energy. A vibrational degree of freedom contributes a heat capacity of 1/2 R each for kinetic and potential energy, for a total of R. Now the bond is contributing heat capacity, and (because of storage of energy in potential energy) is contributing more than if the atoms were not bonded. With full thermal excitation of bond vibration, the heat capacity per volume, or per mole of gas <i>molecules</i> approaches seven-thirds that of monatomic gases. Significantly, this is seven-sixths of the monatomic gas value on a mole-of-atoms basis, so this is now a <i>higher</i> heat capacity <i>per atom</i> than the monatomic figure, because the vibrational mode enables for diatomic gases allows an extra degree of <i>potential energy</i> freedom per pair of atoms, which monatomic gases cannot possess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> See <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_temperature" title="Thermodynamic temperature">thermodynamic temperature</a> for more information on translational motions, kinetic (heat) energy, and their relationship to temperature.
</p><p>However, even at these large temperatures where gaseous nitrogen is able to store 7/6<sup>ths</sup> of the energy <i>per atom</i> of a monatomic gas (making it more efficient at storing energy on an atomic basis), it still only stores 7/12 <sup>ths</sup> of the maximal per-atom heat capacity of a <i>solid,</i> meaning it is not nearly as efficient at storing thermal energy on an atomic basis, as solid substances can be. This is typical of gases, and results because many of the potential bonds which might be storing potential energy in gaseous nitrogen (as opposed to solid nitrogen) are lacking, because only one of the spatial dimensions for each nitrogen atom offers a bond into which potential energy can be stored without increasing the kinetic energy of the atom. In general, solids are most efficient, on an atomic basis, at storing thermal energy (that is, they have the highest per-atom or per-mole-of-atoms heat capacity).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Per_mole_of_different_units">Per mole of different units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Per mole of different units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Per_mole_of_molecules">Per mole of molecules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Per mole of molecules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>When the specific heat capacity, <i>c</i>, of a material is measured (lowercase <i>c</i> means the unit quantity is in terms of mass), different values arise because different substances have different <a href="/wiki/Molar_mass" title="Molar mass">molar masses</a> (essentially, the weight of the individual atoms or molecules). In solids, thermal energy arises due to the number of atoms that are vibrating. "Molar" heat capacity <i>per mole of molecules</i>, for both gases and solids, offer figures which are arbitrarily large, since molecules may be arbitrarily large. Such heat capacities are thus not intensive quantities for this reason, since the quantity of mass being considered can be increased without limit.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Per_mole_of_atoms">Per mole of atoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Per mole of atoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Conversely, for <i>molecular-based</i> substances (which also absorb heat into their internal degrees of freedom), massive, complex molecules with high atomic count—like octane—can store a great deal of energy per mole and yet are quite unremarkable on a mass basis, or on a per-atom basis. This is because, in fully excited systems, heat is stored independently by each atom in a substance, not primarily by the bulk motion of molecules.
</p><p>Thus, it is the heat capacity per-mole-of-atoms, not per-mole-of-molecules, which is the intensive quantity, and which comes closest to being a constant for all substances at high temperatures. This relationship was noticed empirically in 1819, and is called the <a href="/wiki/Dulong%E2%80%93Petit_law" title="Dulong–Petit law">Dulong–Petit law</a>, after its two discoverers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> Historically, the fact that specific heat capacities are approximately equal when corrected by the presumed weight of the atoms of solids, was an important piece of data in favor of the atomic theory of matter.
</p><p>Because of the connection of heat capacity to the number of atoms, some care should be taken to specify a mole-of-molecules basis vs. a mole-of-atoms basis, when comparing specific heat capacities of molecular solids and gases. Ideal gases have the same numbers of molecules per volume, so increasing molecular complexity adds heat capacity on a per-volume and per-mole-of-molecules basis, but may lower or raise heat capacity on a per-atom basis, depending on whether the temperature is sufficient to store energy as atomic vibration.
</p><p>In solids, the quantitative limit of heat capacity in general is about 3 <i>R</i> per mole of atoms, where <i>R</i> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ideal_gas_constant" title="Ideal gas constant">ideal gas constant</a>. This 3 <i>R</i> value is about 24.9 J/mole.K. Six degrees of freedom (three kinetic and three potential) are available to each atom. Each of these six contributes ​<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span>R specific heat capacity per mole of atoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> This limit of 3 <i>R</i> per mole specific heat capacity is approached at room temperature for most solids, with significant departures at this temperature only for solids composed of the lightest atoms which are bound very strongly, such as <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a> (where the value is only of 66% of 3 <i>R</i>), or diamond (where it is only 24% of 3 <i>R</i>). These large departures are due to quantum effects which prevent full distribution of heat into all vibrational modes, when the energy difference between vibrational quantum states is very large compared to the average energy available to each atom from the ambient temperature.
</p><p>For monatomic gases, the specific heat is only half of 3 <i>R</i> per mole, i.e. (​<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>3</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span>R per mole) due to loss of all potential energy degrees of freedom in these gases. For polyatomic gases, the heat capacity will be intermediate between these values on a per-mole-of-atoms basis, and (for heat-stable molecules) would approach the limit of 3 <i>R</i> per mole of atoms, for gases composed of complex molecules, and at higher temperatures at which all vibrational modes accept excitational energy. This is because very large and complex gas molecules may be thought of as relatively large blocks of solid matter which have lost only a relatively small fraction of degrees of freedom, as compared to a fully integrated solid.
</p><p>For a list of heat capacities per atom-mole of various substances, in terms of R, see the last column of the table of heat capacities below.
</p>
<h4><span id="Corollaries_of_these_considerations_for_solids_.28volume-specific_heat_capacity.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Corollaries_of_these_considerations_for_solids_(volume-specific_heat_capacity)">Corollaries of these considerations for solids (volume-specific heat capacity)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Corollaries of these considerations for solids (volume-specific heat capacity)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Since<sup> </sup>the <a href="/wiki/Bulk_density" title="Bulk density">bulk density</a> of a solid chemical element is strongly related to its molar mass (usually about 3 <i>R</i> per mole, as noted above), there exists a noticeable inverse correlation between a solid’s density and its specific heat capacity on a per-mass basis. This is due to a very approximate tendency of atoms of most elements to be about the same size (and constancy of mole-specific heat capacity) resulting in a good correlation between the <i>volume</i> of any given solid chemical element and its total heat capacity. Another way of stating this, is that the volume-specific heat capacity (<a href="/wiki/Volumetric_heat_capacity" title="Volumetric heat capacity">volumetric heat capacity</a>) of solid elements is roughly a constant. The <a href="/wiki/Molar_volume" title="Molar volume">molar volume</a> of solid elements is very roughly constant, and (even more reliably) so also is the molar heat capacity for most solid substances. These two factors determine the volumetric heat capacity, which as a bulk property may be striking in consistency. For example, the element uranium is a metal which has a density almost 36 times that of the metal lithium, but uranium's specific heat capacity on a volumetric basis (i.e. per given volume of metal) is only 18% larger than lithium's.
</p><p>Since the volume-specific corollary of the Dulong–Petit specific heat capacity relationship requires that atoms of all elements take up (on average) the same volume in solids, there are many departures from it, with most of these due to variations in atomic size. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, which is only 14.5% less dense than <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>, has nearly 59% more specific heat capacity on a mass basis. In other words; even though an ingot of arsenic is only about 17% larger than an antimony one of the same mass, it absorbs about 59% more heat for a given temperature rise. The heat capacity ratios of the two substances closely follows the ratios of their molar volumes (the ratios of numbers of atoms in the same volume of each substance); the departure from the correlation to simple volumes in this case is due to lighter arsenic atoms being significantly more closely packed than antimony atoms, instead of similar size. In other words, similar-sized atoms would cause a mole of arsenic to be 63% larger than a mole of antimony, with a correspondingly lower density, allowing its volume to more closely mirror its heat capacity behavior.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_factors">Other factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Other factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen_bonds">Hydrogen bonds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Hydrogen bonds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Hydrogen-containing <a href="/wiki/Chemical_polarity" title="Chemical polarity">polar</a> molecules like <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> have powerful, intermolecular <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonds</a> when in their liquid phase. These bonds provide another place where heat may be stored as potential energy of vibration, even at comparatively low temperatures. Hydrogen bonds account for the fact that liquid water stores nearly the theoretical limit of 3 <i>R</i> per mole of atoms, even at relatively low temperatures (i.e. near the freezing point of water).
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Impurities">Impurities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Impurities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In the case of alloys, there are several conditions in which small impurity concentrations can greatly affect the specific heat. Alloys may exhibit marked difference in behaviour even in the case of small amounts of impurities being one element of the alloy; for example impurities in semiconducting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic" title="Ferromagnetic">ferromagnetic</a> alloys may lead to quite different specific heat properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_simple_case_of_the_monatomic_gas">The simple case of the monatomic gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: The simple case of the monatomic gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the case of a monatomic gas such as <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> under constant volume, if it is assumed that no electronic or nuclear quantum excitations occur, each atom in the gas has only 3 <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(physics_and_chemistry)" title="Degrees of freedom (physics and chemistry)">degrees of freedom</a>, all of a translational type. No energy dependence is associated with the degrees of freedom that define the position of the atoms. While, in fact, the degrees of freedom corresponding to the <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momenta</a> of the atoms are quadratic, and thus contribute to the heat capacity. There are <i>N</i> atoms, each of which has 3 components of momentum, which leads to 3<i>N</i> total degrees of freedom. This gives
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diatomic_gas">Diatomic gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Diatomic gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DiatomicSpecHeat1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1799" data-file-width="2398" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/DiatomicSpecHeat1.png/300px-DiatomicSpecHeat1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/DiatomicSpecHeat1.png/450px-DiatomicSpecHeat1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/DiatomicSpecHeat1.png/600px-DiatomicSpecHeat1.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DiatomicSpecHeat1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Constant-volume specific heat capacity of a diatomic gas (idealised). As temperature increases, heat capacity goes from 3/2 <i>R</i> (translation contribution only), to 5/2 <i>R</i> (translation plus rotation), finally to a maximum of 7/2 <i>R</i> (translation + rotation + vibration)</div></div></div>
<p>In the somewhat more complex case of an ideal gas of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diatomic_molecules" title="Diatomic molecules">diatomic molecules</a>, the presence of internal degrees of freedom are apparent. In addition to the three translational degrees of freedom, there are rotational and vibrational degrees of freedom. In general, the number of degrees of freedom, <i>f</i>, in a molecule with <i>n</i><sub>a</sub> atoms is 3<i>n</i><sub>a</sub>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle f=3n_{\text{a}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>f</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>3</mn>
<msub>
<mi>n</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>a</mtext>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f=3n_{\text{a}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle f=3n_{\text{a}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/36a2b94f4ecbd5d86c6cdb8df573aafbe012f3e6" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.635ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>Mathematically, there are a total of three rotational degrees of freedom, one corresponding to rotation about each of the axes of three-dimensional space. However, in practice only the existence of two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degrees_of_rotational_freedom" title="Degrees of rotational freedom">degrees of rotational freedom</a> for linear molecules will be considered. This approximation is valid because the moment of inertia about the internuclear axis is vanishingly small with respect to other moments of inertia in the molecule (this is due to the very small rotational moments of single atoms, due to the concentration of almost all their mass at their centers; compare also the extremely small radii of the atomic nuclei compared to the distance between them in a diatomic molecule). Quantum mechanically, it can be shown that the interval between successive rotational energy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eigenstates" title="Eigenstates">eigenstates</a> is inversely proportional to the moment of inertia about that axis. Because the moment of inertia about the internuclear axis is vanishingly small relative to the other two rotational axes, the energy spacing can be considered so high that no excitations of the rotational state can occur unless the temperature is extremely high. It is easy to calculate the expected number of vibrational degrees of freedom (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vibrational_modes" title="Vibrational modes">vibrational modes</a>). There are three degrees of translational freedom and two degrees of rotational freedom, therefore
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle f_{\text{vib}}=f-f_{\text{trans}}-f_{\text{rot}}=6-3-2=1.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>vib</mtext>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>f</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>trans</mtext>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<msub>
<mi>f</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>rot</mtext>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>6</mn>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>3</mn>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>1.</mn>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f_{\text{vib}}=f-f_{\text{trans}}-f_{\text{rot}}=6-3-2=1.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle f_{\text{vib}}=f-f_{\text{trans}}-f_{\text{rot}}=6-3-2=1.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4d3edeb35177ab1268e6fc6b2800822f4331318" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:39.36ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>Each rotational and translational degree of freedom will contribute <i>R</i>/2 in the total molar heat capacity of the gas. Each vibrational mode will contribute <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle R}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>R</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle R}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="R" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4b0bfb3769bf24d80e15374dc37b0441e2616e33" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.764ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> to the total molar heat capacity, however. This is because for each vibrational mode, there is a potential and kinetic energy component. Both the potential and kinetic components will contribute <i>R</i>/2 to the total molar heat capacity of the gas. Therefore, a diatomic molecule would be expected to have a molar constant-volume heat capacity of
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C_{V,m}={\frac {3R}{2}}+R+R={\frac {7R}{2}}=3.5R,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>V</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>m</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mn>3</mn>
<mi>R</mi>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>R</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>R</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mn>7</mn>
<mi>R</mi>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>3.5</mn>
<mi>R</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C_{V,m}={\frac {3R}{2}}+R+R={\frac {7R}{2}}=3.5R,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle C_{V,m}={\frac {3R}{2}}+R+R={\frac {7R}{2}}=3.5R,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f6fd663b79c91793cde84349f44a4ad16d918c3c" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:36.47ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where the terms originate from the translational, rotational, and vibrational degrees of freedom respectively.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DiatomicSpecHeat2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1799" data-file-width="2398" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/DiatomicSpecHeat2.png/300px-DiatomicSpecHeat2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/DiatomicSpecHeat2.png/450px-DiatomicSpecHeat2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/DiatomicSpecHeat2.png/600px-DiatomicSpecHeat2.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DiatomicSpecHeat2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Constant-volume specific heat capacity of diatomic gases (real gases) between about 200 K and 2000 K. This temperature range is not large enough to include both quantum transitions in all gases. Instead, at 200 K, all but hydrogen are fully rotationally excited, so all have at least 5/2 <i>R</i> heat capacity. (Hydrogen is already below 5/2, but it will require cryogenic conditions for even H<sub>2</sub> to fall to 3/2 <i>R</i>). Further, only the heavier gases fully reach 7/2 <i>R</i> at the highest temperature, due to the relatively small vibrational energy spacing of these molecules. HCl and H<sub>2</sub> begin to make the transition above 500 K, but have not achieved it by 1000 K, since their vibrational energy level spacing is too wide to fully participate in heat capacity, even at this temperature.</div></div></div>

</p>

<p>From the above table, clearly there is a problem with the above theory. All of the diatomics examined have heat capacities that are lower than those predicted by the <a href="/wiki/Equipartition_theorem" title="Equipartition theorem">equipartition theorem</a>, except Br<sub>2</sub>. However, as the atoms composing the molecules become heavier, the heat capacities move closer to their expected values. One of the reasons for this phenomenon is the quantization of vibrational, and to a lesser extent, rotational states. In fact, if it is assumed that the molecules remain in their lowest-energy vibrational state because the inter-level energy spacings for vibration energies are large, the predicted molar constant-volume heat capacity for a diatomic molecule becomes just that from the contributions of translation and rotation:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle C_{V,m}={\frac {3R}{2}}+R={\frac {5R}{2}}=2.5R,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>V</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>m</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mn>3</mn>
<mi>R</mi>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>R</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mn>5</mn>
<mi>R</mi>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>2.5</mn>
<mi>R</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle C_{V,m}={\frac {3R}{2}}+R={\frac {5R}{2}}=2.5R,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle C_{V,m}={\frac {3R}{2}}+R={\frac {5R}{2}}=2.5R,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cf1a33b60b43459c501fa07f9bd57b7fabbc5f97" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:31.866ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>which is a fairly close approximation of the heat capacities of the lighter molecules in the above table. If the quantum <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_oscillator" title="Harmonic oscillator">harmonic oscillator</a> approximation is made, it turns out that the quantum vibrational energy level spacings are actually inversely proportional to the square root of the <a href="/wiki/Reduced_mass" title="Reduced mass">reduced mass</a> of the atoms composing the diatomic molecule. Therefore, in the case of the heavier diatomic molecules such as chlorine or bromine, the quantum vibrational energy-level spacings become finer, which allows more excitations into higher vibrational levels at lower temperatures. This limit for storing heat capacity in vibrational modes, as discussed above, becomes 7<i>R</i>/2 = 3.5 <i>R</i> per mole of gas molecules, which is fairly consistent with the measured value for Br<sub>2</sub> at room temperature. As temperatures rise, all diatomic gases approach this value.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_gas_phase">General gas phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: General gas phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Heat_capacity&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(February 2015)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The specific heat of the gas is best conceptualized in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(physics_and_chemistry)" title="Degrees of freedom (physics and chemistry)">degrees of freedom</a> of an individual molecule. The different degrees of freedom correspond to the different ways in which the molecule may store energy. The molecule may store energy in its translational motion according to the formula:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle E={\frac {1}{2}}\,m\left(v_{x}^{2}+v_{y}^{2}+v_{z}^{2}\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="E=\frac{1}{2}\,m\left(v_x^2+v_y^2+v_z^2\right)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/29489c02880c6dabac89a33e78de9a147cfec0cf" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:24.162ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where <i>m</i>  is the mass of the molecule and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle [v_{x},v_{y},v_{z}]}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</msub>

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle [v_{x},v_{y},v_{z}]}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="[v_x,v_y,v_z]" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/61a890598143c1f2deb07aa3133a853eb24afcc7" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:9.968ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> is velocity of the center of mass of the molecule. Each direction of motion constitutes a degree of freedom, thus there are three translational degrees of freedom.
</p><p>In addition, a molecule may have rotational motion. The kinetic energy of rotational motion is generally expressed as
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle E={\frac {1}{2}}\,\left(I_{1}\omega _{1}^{2}+I_{2}\omega _{2}^{2}+I_{3}\omega _{3}^{2}\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>where <i>I</i>  is the <a href="/wiki/Moment_of_inertia" title="Moment of inertia">moment of inertia</a> tensor of the molecule, and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle [\omega _{1},\omega _{2},\omega _{3}]}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
